$C_p = 14 + 0.04T$ [empirically estimated from $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ and $\mathrm{KBF_4}$ (A. S. Dworkin, MSR Program Semiann Progr. Rept. Aug. 31, 1968, ORNL-4344, p. 157)]

![](images/875a804e3c862eae4969fc8ae7928f66c2adab517eb2f66f92c9d3354eaa5230.jpg)  
Fig. 12.1. Decomposition Pressures of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ from the Reaction $\mathrm{LiBF}_4(c) = \mathrm{LiF}(c) + \mathrm{BF}_3(g)$ .

mole $^{-1}$ ; a value of $-36.88$ would have led to $\Delta H_{298}^{\circ} = 21.4 \, \text{kcal/mole}$ .

The second-law analysis of the data yielded $\Delta H_{298}^{\circ} = 21.1\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ , in excellent agreement with the calorimetric value. To calculate the second-law $\Delta H_{298}^{\circ}$ , we used $\Delta H_{535}^{\circ} = 19.34\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ ( $535^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ is the midpoint of the experimental temperature range) together with the heat capacities given at the bottom of Table 12.7.

As stated earlier, one of the purposes of this investigation was to derive $S_{298}^{\circ}$ for $\mathrm{LiBF}_4(c)$ . The data of this investigation, the calorimetrically derived $\Delta H_{298}^{\circ}$ , and other thermochemical data were combined to yield $S_{298}^{\circ}$ of $\mathrm{LiBF}_4(c)$ using the equation:

$$
\begin{array}{l} S _ {2 9 8} ^ {\circ} \left[ \operatorname {L i B F} _ {4} (c) \right] = S _ {2 9 8} ^ {\circ} \left[ \operatorname {L i F} (c) \right] + S _ {2 9 8} ^ {\circ} \left[ \mathrm {B F} _ {3} (g) \right] \\ - R \ln P _ {\mathrm {B F} _ {3}} - \frac {\Delta H _ {2 9 8} ^ {c}}{T} + \int_ {2 9 8. 1 5} ^ {T} \Delta C _ {p} d \ln T \\ - \frac {1}{T} \int_ {2 9 8. 1 5} ^ {T} \Delta C _ {p} d T, \tag {2} \\ \end{array}
$$

where $\Delta H_{298}^{\circ} = 21.40\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ and $\Delta C_p$ is $C_p[\mathrm{LiF}(c)] + C_p[\mathrm{BF}_3(g)] - C_p[\mathrm{LiBF}_4(c)]$ . Equation (2) was solved (13 times) for each pressure-temperature pair listed in Table 12.7. To calculate the uncertainty in $S_{298}^{\circ}[\mathrm{LiBF}_4(c)]$ , only the uncertainties in the first four terms on the right-hand side of Eq. (2) were considered; that is, it was assumed that the uncertainties in the last

two terms (containing $\Delta C_p$ ) would cancel each other. By Eq. (2) the result obtained was:

$$
S _ {2 9 8} ^ {\circ} \left[ \operatorname {L i B F} _ {4} (c) \right] = 3 4. 0 \pm 0. 4 \text {c a l} \deg^ {- 1} \text {m o l e} ^ {- 1},
$$

where the $\pm 0.4$ is the square root of the sum of the squares of the uncertainties in the first four terms of Eq. (2) (respectively $0.04,140.3,140.055,0.18^{11}$ cal deg $^{-1}$ mole $^{-1}$ ).

# 12.6 X-RAY CRYSTAL PARAMETERS OF $\mathsf{LiBF}_4$

G. D. Brunton S. Cantor

To obtain a reasonably reliable value for the density of crystalline $\mathrm{LiBF_4}$ , the powder diffraction pattern was measured with the salt enclosed in a glass capillary. Cell parameters were derived by assigning Miller indices to the observed lines from the comparable reflections of the $\mathrm{LiClO_4}$ structure.[15] $\mathrm{LiBF_4}$ is orthorhombic, with a tetramolecular unit cell of dimensions:

$$
a = 8. 6 5 \pm 0. 0 8 \mathrm {A},
$$

$$
b = 6. 8 4 \pm 0. 0 7 \mathrm {A},
$$

$$
c = 4. 8 4 \pm 0. 0 3 \mathrm {A}.
$$

The density of $\mathrm{LiBF_4}$ is, therefore, $2.17\pm 0.06$ $\mathrm{g / cm^3}$

The above results should be considered as a preliminary measurement since there are some uncertainties about the purity and crystal size of the sample.

$^{14}$ K. K. Kelley and E. G. King, U.S. Bureau of Mines Bulletin 592, pp. 103, 109 (1961).

15 R. E. Gluyas, Dissertation Abstr. 17, 2800 (1959).

# 12.7 MIXING REACTIONS OF MSBR FUEL AND COOLANT SALTS

D.M.Moulton J.H.Shaffer

It has been known for some time that there is a wide range of liquid-liquid immiscibility in the system of $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ and $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ and that the fluorides of uranium and the trivalent lanthanides are insoluble in the latter salt.16 There seemed to be a possibility, however, that divalent rare earths would distribute to some extent into the fluoroborate phase, since it is a good acceptor of ions of low charge density, and that this might find use in a reprocessing scheme.

To test this, $0.98\mathrm{kg}$ of $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ (68-20-12 mole %) with $0.75\mathrm{g}$ of $\mathrm{EuF_3}$ and $7\mathrm{g}$ of $\mathrm{UF_4}$ was mixed with $1.61\mathrm{kg}$ of $\mathrm{NaBF_4 - NaF}$ (92-8 mole %) at $538^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . An $\mathrm{Ar - 3\% BF_3}$ sparge gas was used to establish a $\mathrm{BF_3}$ pressure. Three additions of thorium metal were made to reduce the europium to the divalent state. There was no way to see the phase separation directly, but the analyses of the dipper samples of the two salts showed very different compositions. Data for this experiment are shown in Table 12.8. As expected, the extraction of both uranium and europium increased on reduction, but neither got high enough to be very useful in processing. The increased thorium distribution is hard to explain. The very large amount of beryllium moving to the fluoroborate despite its high charge density suggests a bona fide $\mathrm{BeF_4}^{2 - }$ ion in that salt.

$^{16}$ C. E. Bamberger et al., MSR Program Semiann. Progr. Rept. Feb. 29, 1968, ORNL-4254, p. 171.

Table 12.8. Mixing Reactions of MSBR Fuel and Coolant Salts Mole fractions are calculated on the basis of taking the components as LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ -NaF-BF $_3$ : coolant is NaF-BF $_3$ (52-48 mole %)   

<table><tr><td>Sample</td><td>NLiF</td><td>NBef2</td><td>NNaF</td><td>NTf4</td><td>NUF4</td><td>NBF3</td><td>DEu</td><td>Sum</td><td>Th Added (g)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>×10-3</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>0.498</td><td>0.117</td><td>0.257</td><td>0.110</td><td>1.3</td><td>0.0185</td><td>0.0044</td><td>0.002</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><td>2-3</td><td>0.094</td><td>0.045</td><td>0.430</td><td>0.003</td><td>0.08</td><td>0.428</td><td></td><td>-0.008</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>4-5</td><td>0.486</td><td>0.104</td><td>0.242</td><td>0.085</td><td>1.1</td><td>0.082</td><td>0.0080</td><td>0.051</td><td>2.3</td></tr><tr><td>6-7</td><td>0.099</td><td>0.050</td><td>0.434</td><td>0.004</td><td>0.08</td><td>0.414</td><td></td><td>0.001</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>8-9</td><td>0.428</td><td>0.119</td><td>0.270</td><td>0.092</td><td>1.0</td><td>0.090</td><td>0.036</td><td>-0.049</td><td>7.3</td></tr><tr><td>10-11</td><td>0.131</td><td>0.058</td><td>0.430</td><td>0.009</td><td>0.15</td><td>0.371</td><td></td><td>0.033</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>12-13</td><td>0.438</td><td>0.108</td><td>0.272</td><td>0.085</td><td>1.0</td><td>0.097</td><td>0.034</td><td>0.010</td><td>12.3</td></tr><tr><td>14-15</td><td>0.125</td><td>0.059</td><td>0.428</td><td>0.009</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.379</td><td></td><td>0.015</td><td></td></tr></table>

The most interesting observation in the experiment is not the extraction but rather the mixing behavior of the two salts. As soon as the salts were mixed, the total pressure rose to 12 psig, presumably by $\mathrm{BF}_3$ evolution. The pressure was reduced periodically during the experiment by bleed-off but rose regularly to at least 2-3 psig when the system was closed. There appears to be a very substantial destabilization of the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion when Li, Be, or Th ions enter the fluoroborate phase, caused, for example, by such reactions as

$$
\mathrm {B e F} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {B F} _ {4} ^ {-} \rightarrow \mathrm {B e F} _ {4} ^ {2 -} + 2 \mathrm {B F} _ {3},
$$

although there is no way to distinguish the individual effects of the three cations. Probably all of them have a destabilizing effect, and for lithium and thorium, at least, it is questionable whether the effect is caused by the formation of a specific complex species.

Bamberger found in thorium-free systems that the equality

$$
N _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + N _ {\mathrm {N a F}} = N _ {\mathrm {B F} _ {3}} + 2 N _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}
$$

held reasonably well and suggested that the system might be regarded as a reciprocal mixture of $\mathbf{Na}^+$ , $\mathbf{Li}^+$ , $\mathbf{BF}_4^-$ , and $\mathbf{BeF}_2^{2-}$ . In our case the relationship works only if the term $4.5(N_{\mathbf{ThF}_4} + N_{\mathbf{UF}_4})$ is added to the right-hand side. We have shown the quantity $N_{\mathbf{LiF}} + N_{\mathbf{NaF}} - N_{\mathbf{BF}_3} - 2N_{\mathbf{BeF}_2} - 4.5(N_{\mathbf{ThF}_4} + N_{\mathbf{UF}_4})$ as "Sum" in the table. The coefficient 4.5, which works better than either 4 or 5, implies that the average coordination number for thorium is 8.5. The data from this experiment do not allow distinguishing between a "physical" (Coulombic) and a "chemical" (complexing) mechanism for $\mathbf{BF}_3$ displacement, and in any case the greatest difference between these may be semantic.

Development of a leak in the MSBR heat exchanger and subsequent mixing of fuel and coolant salt would result in an increase in the partial pressure of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ over the salt mixture. Smith and Smith $^{17}$ found that unexpectedly high pressures were required to suppress cavitation in operation of a fluoroborate test loop that had previously contained LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ -UF $_4$ . Contamination of the fluoroborate salt by small amounts of residual LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ -UF $_4$ undoubtedly accounted for the unexpectedly high vapor pressure above the salt. On replacing the salt with a clean charge the pressure returned to normal. Any leakage of coolant into the fuel salt would cause similar behavior at the fuel pump,

since $\mathrm{BF}_3$ is not very soluble in the MSBR fuel salt.

The degree of contamination of the fuel salt by heat exchanger leakage is hard to assess. NaF will dissolve readily. $\mathrm{BF}_3$ will be quite volatile and should strip out, but whether it gets to a high enough rate to affect nuclear performance must be a function of the leak and stripping rates, of which the latter cannot yet be calculated. Copious amounts of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ could emerge in the gas stream. Gross contamination would require reprocessing of the salt streams, possibly by increasing the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure and lowering the temperature to give maximum phase separation. Of the fission products, one would expect Rb and Cs, and possibly some of the Sr and Ba, to go to the fluoroborate phase, while rare earths, actinides, Zr, and other highly charged species would remain in the fuel salt. Most of the uranium should be recoverable in this way. Further studies of the fluoride-fluoroborate system are in progress.

# 12.8 PHASE RELATIONS IN THE SYSTEM NaF-KF-BF3

C.J.Barton L.O.Gilpatrick H.Insley

We reported earlier $^{18}$ that we had been unable to find the minimum-melting composition in the system NaF-KF-BF $_3$ that theoretically should exist on the boundary between the NaF and the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -KBF $_4$ solid solution fields. At this composition the liquidus and solidus temperatures should coincide as they do at the minimum melting composition in the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -KBF $_4$ quasi-binary system. We continued this search, concentrating our effort on compositions near the NaF-NaBF $_4$ binary eutectic and the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -KBF $_4$ minimum. The DTA data in this part of the system, including some information previously reported, $^{18}$ are displayed in Table 12.9.

These data show that there is a small region defined by the limits (in mole %) $48.5 \pm 1.0 \mathrm{NaF}, 3.5 \pm 0.5 \mathrm{KF}$ , and $48.0 \pm 0.5 \mathrm{BF}_3$ , where the liquidus and solidus temperatures are essentially constant with an average spread of $5^{\circ}$ between the two values. It should be noted that quenching data for several compositions in or near this composition range show liquid persisting below $372^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ , but the amount is believed to be small and may be attributed to the presence of a small amount of oxygen in the system. This effect may also help to account for our failure to find a composition with a

Table 12.9 DTA Data for the System NaF-KF-BF3

smaller spread between liquidus and solidus temperatures. We have observed that the purest $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ that we have been able to produce melted over a range of $10^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ when heated in the DTA apparatus under the same conditions used to obtain the data in Table 12.9, and this compound makes up a large fraction of all these preparations. We believe that further study of this system is not likely to reveal a composition melting at a temperature significantly lower than that of the $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ eutectic composition $(384^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . A phase diagram for this system that incorporates information

obtained since the publication of an earlier version $^{19}$ is shown in Fig. 12.2.

# 12.9 PREPARATION OF PURE $\mathbf{NaBF}_4$

C.J.Barton L.O.Gilpatrick

Part of our fluoroborate study has been devoted to preparation of pure $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ because we need pure material for phase studies and preparation of an oxygen-free product has not been easily achieved. The technique that gave the highest-melting compound yet prepared, passing a mixture of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ , HF, and helium through molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ , was used to prepare another batch of this compound. This time the entire $\mathrm{BF}_3$ -HF treatment was carried out at $425^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , whereas in the earlier trial the treatment was performed first at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and then at $425^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The resulting product melted at $406^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , as compared with $408^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for the earlier preparation. It appears that a higher temperature or more prolonged treatment at the lower temperature may be required for complete oxygen removal. The product was stored in a glass bottle after grinding to pass an 80-mesh sieve. After several months the bottle was

$^{19}$ MSR Program Semiann. Progr. Rept. Feb. 29, 1968, ORNL-4254, p. 166.

![](images/6eaa21df76750985dd29c39f240e6828d462c8767ee26160b2b168d4279516ec.jpg)  
Fig. 12.2. The System NaF-KF-BF $_3$ .

observed to be etched, and the powder had caked quite badly. We believe that HF or free $\mathbf{BF}_3$ remaining in the product after cooling to room temperature was responsible for both phenomena.

We found earlier that recrystallization of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ from 0.5 or $1M$ HF solution gave a fairly pure product. In an effort to produce oxygen-free $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ we dissolved $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ recrystallized from dilute HF in concentrated $(48\%)$ HF. Chemical analysis of the first crop of crystals that separated from the cold concentrated HF solution showed it to be a mixture of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ and NaF-HF.

One portion of a subsequent crop of crystals, after heating under a vacuum at about $150^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , gave an analysis that corresponded closely to the theoretical values for $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . The oxygen content of this material was 200 ppm, as low as any $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ prepared to date, but it was reported to contain 500 ppm $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ . The other portion of this crop contained 340 ppm oxygen and 300 ppm $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ after 24 hr in a vacuum desiccator at room temperature. This technique may warrant further investigation for the laboratory-scale preparation of pure $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ .

# 12.10 ATTEMPTED SYNTHESIS OF $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$

L. O. Gilpatrick C. J. Barton

Hydroxyfluoroborates are of interest because they are hydrolysis products of the tetrafluoroborates and they will undoubtedly increase the corrosiveness of fluoroborate coolant materials. The successful synthesis of $\mathrm{KBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ by the reaction

$$
2 \mathrm {K H F} _ {2} + \mathrm {H} _ {3} \mathrm {B O} _ {3} \xrightarrow {\circ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} \mathrm {K B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + \mathrm {K F} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}
$$

was reported earlier.20 We attempted to make $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ by a similar technique, but the product contained two phases in approximately equal amounts. One was $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and the other had a higher refractive index and was assumed to be the desired product, $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ . We attempted to separate the two products by dissolving the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ in hot acetone or a mixture of acetone and water, but the separation was not complete. We then found that addition of an organic amine to hot acetone resulted in complete solution of the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , but a small amount of NaF was present in the residue. This was removed by dissolving the product in a small amount of water, filtering off the slightly soluble NaF, and recrystallizing the product by slowly evaporating the water. The resulting product appeared to be single-phase material when examined under the microscope. It had a refractive index of about 1.355, as compared to 1.305 for $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . Its chemical analysis corresponded very closely to the empirical formula $\mathrm{NaBF}_2\mathrm{O}$ . When heated for the first time in a sealed nickel capsule in the DTA apparatus, a large thermal effect was observed at about $408^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , near the melting point of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , but subsequent thermal cycling revealed no significant thermal effects. When the capsule was opened at room temperature and examined under the microscope, the material was found to consist of NaF embedded in a glassy, water-soluble phase. Efforts to use infrared absorption to identify the $\mathrm{NaBF}_2\mathrm{O}$ compound, a formulation that does not appear to exist in the literature, have not as yet produced useful results. We plan to try other methods of making $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ , and we will also try to characterize the product we made, since it has apparently not been previously reported.

20C. J. Barton, L. O. Gilpatrick, and H. Insley, MSR Program Semiann. Progr. Rept. Aug. 31, 1968, ORNL-4344, p. 156.

# 13. Physical Chemistry of Molten Salts

# 13.1 PHASE RELATIONS IN THE SYSTEM LiF-BeF2-CeF3

C. J. Barton

L. O. Gilpatrick

H. Insley

As part of a long-range study of phase relations in the system $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4 - CeF_3}$ , which will allow prediction of the behavior of $\mathrm{PuF_3}$ in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ mixtures, we prepared nine mixtures in the system $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - CeF_3}$ . Our immediate objective was to establish the temperature and composition of the $\mathrm{BeF_2 - CeF_3}$ eutectic, but the viscosity of $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ -rich compositions at temperatures below the melting point of $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ ( $555^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) is believed to be too high to permit ready attainment of equilibrium. Thoma et al. made small additions of NaF to $\mathrm{BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ mixtures to lower the viscosity and to speed the attainment of equilibrium in the determination of the $\mathrm{BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ eutectic temperature and composition, but we chose to add LiF rather than NaF because of the need to establish phase relations in the ternary system $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - CeF_3}$ . The $\mathrm{CeF_3}$ solubility work of Ward et al. established liquidus temperatures for compositions in this system containing 28.5 to 48.4 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ .

The mixtures were prepared by weighing the required amounts of ground, distilled $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , LiF, and $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ into graphite crucibles, adding $\mathrm{NH}_4\mathrm{F}\cdot \mathrm{HF}$ , and heating to about $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in a flowing helium atmosphere. Some of the $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ distilled out of the crucibles during this process, and all the fused mixtures were weighed after cooling to room temperature in order to measure the loss in weight before grinding. Since LiF and $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ have low vapor pressures at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the weight loss was assumed to be due entirely to evaporation of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . Analyses of two compositions now available do not agree with the values calculated on this assumption.

Portions of the ground mixtures were sealed in gradient quench tubes and heated for 21 days before quenching. The results of this preliminary investigation can be summarized as follows:

1. $\mathbf{BeF}_2$ was observed as the primary phase (at $547^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) in only one mixture, LiF- $\mathbf{BeF}_2$ - $\mathbf{CeF}_3$ (0.6-97.9-1.5 mole % by analysis).   
2. Liquidus temperatures in the $\mathbf{CeF}_3$ primary phase

field increase rapidly with increasing $\mathbf{CeF}_3$ content, as might be expected from the very high melting point $(1460^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ of $\mathbf{CeF}_3$ .

3. The temperature of appearance of two crystalline phases $(\mathrm{CeF}_3$ and $\mathrm{BeF}_2)$ in equilibrium with liquid was surprisingly reproducible, falling in the range $538 \pm 3^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for seven mixtures. This is probably near the $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ - $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ eutectic temperature.   
4. Evidence of liquid immiscibility (two glasses) was noted in two compositions, that listed under item 1 above and $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - CeF_3}$ (0.5-86.1-9.5 mole % by analysis), in the approximate temperature range 570 to $540^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

Tests now under way will show whether equilibrium in $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -rich $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ - $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ compositions can be achieved by a long equilibration period.

# 13.2 HETEROGENEOUS EQUILIBRIA BETWEEN CERIUM-CONTAINING MOLTEN FLUORIDES AND OXIDE PHASES

C.E.Bamberger C.F.Baes,Jr.

In search of an alternative method for removal of rare-earth fission products from MSBR fuels, we have begun a series of experiments to synthesize sparingly soluble compounds containing rare earths which could act as selective ion exchangers for such fission products. The initial tests have involved as starting materials various oxides which our experience and/or available thermochemical data suggest might be stable or nearly

stable in molten fluorides and which might react with rare-earth ions in the molten fluoride phase to form a rare-earth-containing oxide phase. Any such rare-earth compound so formed would, per se, be compatible with the molten fluoride phase and might well serve as a rare-earth ion exchanger.

The formation of a double oxide in the presence of an $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ melt may be considered in terms of the following reactions:

$$
\mathrm {L n F} _ {3} (d) + \frac {3}{4} \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (c) \rightleftharpoons \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {L n} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {3} (c) + \frac {3}{4} \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (d). \tag {1}
$$

(The presence of $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ as an equilibrium oxide phase should provide the maximum concentration of dissolved oxide.) Available thermochemical data<sup>3</sup> suggest that $\Delta G(1)$ for this reaction is $\sim +35$ kcal for cerium and $\sim +15$ kcal for samarium or europium. It is evident that if the reaction

$$
n \mathrm {M O} _ {z / 2} + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {L n} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {3} \rightarrow \mathrm {L n M} _ {n} \mathrm {O} _ {(n z + 3) / 2} \tag {2}
$$

can provide the needed negative free energy [i.e., $\Delta G(2)$ more negative than $-15$ to $-35\mathrm{kcal}]$ then the double oxide should form by reaction of $\mathbf{MO}_{z / 2}$ , $\mathbf{ThO_2}$ , and dissolved $\mathbf{LnF}_3$ ,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {L n F} _ {3} (d) + n \mathrm {M O} _ {2 / 2} (c) + \frac {3}{4} \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (c) \rightleftharpoons \frac {3}{4} \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (d) \\ + \operatorname {L n M} _ {n} \mathrm {O} _ {(n z + 3) / 2} (c) \tag {3} \\ \end{array}
$$

The high stability required of the double oxide suggests that it should appear in the $\mathrm{MO}_{z/2} - \mathrm{Ln}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ binary system as a congruently melting phase. It is also evident that the oxide $\mathrm{MO}_{z/2}$ must be stable - or nearly so - in the presence of the molten LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ phase; that is, there should be no extensive metathesis reaction to precipitate large amounts of ThO $_2$ ,

$$
\mathrm {M O} _ {z / 2} + \frac {z}{4} \mathrm {T h} ^ {4 +} \Rightarrow \mathrm {M} ^ {z +} + \frac {z}{4} \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (c), \tag {4}
$$

or oxidation reaction, for example, of nickel,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {M O} _ {z / 2} (c) + \frac {z - y}{2} \mathrm {N i} (c) + \frac {y}{4} \mathrm {T h} ^ {4 +} \rightleftharpoons M ^ {y +} \\ + \frac {z - y}{2} \mathrm {N i O} (c) + \frac {y}{4} \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (c). \tag {5} \\ \end{array}
$$

Oxides of chromium, iron, and vanadium were chosen for testing because their oxidation reactions with nickel were predicted not to be extensive (Table 13.1) and because the binary systems $\mathrm{Cr_2O_3 - Ln_2O_3}$ , $\mathrm{Fe_2O_3}$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {M O} _ {z / 2} (c) + \frac {z - y}{2} \mathrm {N i} (c) + \frac {y}{4} \mathrm {T h} ^ {4 +} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {M} ^ {y +} \\ + \frac {(z - y)}{2} \mathrm {N i O} (c) + \frac {y}{4} \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (c) \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
K = X _ {\mathrm {M} ^ {y +}} / \left(X _ {\mathrm {T h} ^ {4 +}}\right) ^ {y / 4}
$$

Table 13.1. Predicted Extent of Reaction of Various Oxides $(\mathrm{MO}_{z / 2})$ with Molten LiF-BeF2-ThF4 (72-16-12 Mole %) Saturated with NiO and $\mathbf{ThO_2}$ and Contained in Nickel at $600^{\circ}C$   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Oxide</td><td rowspan="2">Reaction Product</td><td colspan="2">Calculated Concentrationa</td></tr><tr><td>Mole Fraction</td><td>ppm</td></tr><tr><td>NiO</td><td>·Ni2+</td><td>6 × 10-5</td><td>60</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">Cr2O3</td><td>Cr2+</td><td>4 × 10-8</td><td>0.03</td></tr><tr><td>Cr3+</td><td>5 × 10-8</td><td>0.04</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">Fe3O4</td><td>Fe2+</td><td>0.002</td><td>1800</td></tr><tr><td>Fe3+</td><td>2 × 10-6</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">V2O5</td><td>V2+</td><td>~10-13</td><td>~10-7</td></tr><tr><td>V3+</td><td>~10-11</td><td>~10-5</td></tr><tr><td>Al2O3</td><td>Al3+</td><td>Extensive reaction</td><td></td></tr></table>

$a$ Estimated from the sources of free energy values listed in ref. 3.

$\mathrm{Ln_2O_3}$ , and $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5 - \mathbf{Ln}_2\mathbf{O}_3^4$ all contain congruently melting intermediate compounds. The possible reactions which might produce rare-earth-containing oxides are listed in Table 13.2, reactions 1-3. Note that in the case of $\mathrm{Fe_3O_4}$ , since oxidation is necessary to produce $\mathrm{LnFeO_3}$ , the reaction is favored by an oxidant, for example,NiO.

Silicates were also included in these tests since rare earths often are found associated with the mineral thorite $\left(\mathrm{ThSiO}_2\right)$ . The compounds $\mathrm{ThSiO}_4$ and $\mathrm{SiO}_2$ are expected to be stable phases in the presence of LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ melts at low partial pressures of $\mathrm{SiF}_4$ . Solid solutions of $\mathrm{UO}_2$ - $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ were also tested. In these two instances possible extraction reactions, if any,

Table 13.2. Possible Reactions for Extraction of Rare-Earth Cations from   
Molten LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$   

<table><tr><td>1. 1/2Cr2O3(c) + 3/4ThO2(c) + Ln3+→3/4Th4+ + LnCrO3(c)</td></tr><tr><td>2. 1/3Fe3O4(c) + 3/4ThO2 + 1/6NiO(c) + Ln3+→3/4Th4+ + 1/6Ni(c) + LnFeO3(c)</td></tr><tr><td>3. 1/2V2O5(c or l) + 3/4ThO2(c) + Ln3+→3/4Th4+ + LnVO4(c)</td></tr><tr><td>4.a 1/2Th4+(SiO4) + F- + Ln3+→1/2Th4+ + F-(SiO4) + Ln3+(SiO4)</td></tr><tr><td>5.b 2U4+(O) + Ln3+→U3+ + U5+(O) + Ln3+(O)</td></tr></table>

$a^{\prime \prime}\mathrm{SiO}_4$ denotes an ion in a ThSiO lattice.   
$b^{*}\mathrm{O}^{*}$ denotes an ion in a $\mathrm{UO}_2\text{-ThO}_2$ solid solution.

might occur by replacement of a $\mathbf{Th}^{4 + }$ cation in the silicate or oxide lattice with two $\mathrm{Ce}^{3 + }$ plus two $\mathbf{F}^{-}$ ions (Table 13.2, reaction 4). In the case of the $\mathrm{UO}_2\text{-}\mathrm{ThO}_2$ solid solution, $\mathbf{Ln}^{3 + }$ cations might also enter the oxide lattice by releasing one $\mathbf{U}^{3 + }$ cation and oxidizing one $\mathbf{U}^{4 + }$ lattice cation to $\mathbf{U}^{5 + }$ (Table 13.2, reaction 5).

$\mathrm{Al_2O_3}$ is of interest and was included in these initial tests not only because it forms congruently melting compounds with rare-earth oxides but also because it, along with $\mathrm{SiO_2}$ , is a principal component of zeolite-molecular sieve materials which could act as ion exchangers.

The experimental technique consisted in stirring 0.1 mole or more of the oxide starting material with $\sim 500$ g of fluoride melt of a composition near LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 mole %) in a leak-tight stirred vessel under argon or a mixture of argon- $4\%$ hydrogen. Cerium fluoride, usually 0.05 mole, was added as the representative rare earth because of the convenience of the tracer ${}^{144}\mathrm{Ce}$ which was incorporated into the fluoride mixture together with inert CeF $_3$ . The concentration of cerium in filtered samples was determined radiometrically with a precision of $\pm 3\%$ . Occasionally, samples of the solid phase were taken, and the stable solid compounds present were determined by x-ray and petrographic methods. ThO $_2$ was generally added to supply oxide ions.

The various tests are summarized in Table 13.3:

1. The equilibration of a melt containing $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ with $\mathrm{SiO}_2 + \mathrm{ThSiO}_4$ with $\mathrm{ThSiO}_4 + \mathrm{ThO}_2$ at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ did not produce a detectable cerium removal. The $\mathrm{ThSiO}_4$ was synthesized from $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ and $\mathrm{SiO}_2$ during these equilibrations, confirming the expected stability of this phase.

2. A $\mathrm{UO_2 - ThO_2}$ solid solution dilute in $\mathrm{UO_2}$ seemed to produce a slight removal of cerium from a fluoride phase containing a low concentration of cerium $(X_{\mathrm{CeF}_3} \sim 3 \times 10^{-4})$ . A $\mathrm{UO_2 - ThO_2}$ solid solution rich in $\mathrm{UO_2}$ caused the removal of much more cerium, but after cooling none was found associated with the oxide phase. Similarly, in a subsequent experiment at a higher concentration of cerium in the fluoride melt $(X_{\mathrm{CeF}_3} \sim 7 \times 10^{-3})$ , no cerium removal was detected, and no cerium activity was found in the separated oxide phase, even when NiO was added to favor reaction 5 in Table 13.2. At present we have no explanation for the removal of cerium observed in the earlier test.

3. $\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}$ with or without $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ did not show any cerium extraction (Table 13.2, reaction 1). This oxide was found to be stable, as expected. Indeed, it is probable that the amount of chromium dissolved in the melt (Table 13.3) was much lower than the analytical value because of incomplete removal of $\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}$ from the filtered samples. On the chance that the concentration of chromium in the melt at equilibrium was low enough to cause slow kinetics, $\mathrm{CrF_3}$ was added to precipitate $\mathrm{CeCrO_3}$ from the melt:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {C r F} _ {3} (d) + \mathrm {C e F} _ {3} (d) + \frac {3}{2} \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (c) \rightarrow \mathrm {C e C r O} _ {3} (c) \\ + \frac {3}{2} \operatorname {T h F} _ {4} (d). \tag {6} \\ \end{array}
$$

No detectable amount of cerium was removed.

4. Magnetite, $\mathrm{Fe_3O_4}$ , produced no detectable extraction with or without $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ present. The addition of $\mathrm{NiF_2}$ as an oxidant to promote reaction 2 in Table

Table 13.3. Tests of Rare-Earth Extraction from Molten LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 Mole %) by Various Oxides

${}^{a}$ Numbers indicate millimoles of oxide added per mole (63.2 g) of ${\mathrm{{LiF} - {BeF}}}_{2} - {\mathrm{{ThF}}}_{4}$ (72-16-12 mole %).   
$b_{\text{Phases in brackets}}$ are presumed to be present but were not identified.   
Where not specified the $\mathrm{Ce}^{3+}$ concentration was 0.67 mole%.

13.2 produced a small amount of cerium removal, though again the precipitated phase was not established. With both NiO and $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ present, the amount of $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ found in solution was in excellent agreement with the predicted value (Table 13.1).

5. The equilibration of $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ with a cerium-containing melt together with NiO and $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ (reaction 3, Table 13.2) showed some cerium removal, and then more removal when the amount of $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ was increased and the temperature was raised above the melting point of $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ ( $\sim 670^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). After termination of this run, extensive corrosion of the nickel thermocouple well was found. We attribute this to mass transfer of nickel caused by the formation of an $\mathrm{NiO - V_2O_5}$ liquid phase. In a future test, the addition of NiO will be omitted. The low vanadium concentrations found in samples of the fluoride melt tend to confirm the predicted stability of $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ . Again, these values are thought to be high because of incomplete separation of the $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ phase from the filtered sample.   
6. $\mathrm{Al}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{3}$ was found to react completely with the fluoride melt to produce solid $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ . Analysis of

filtered samples showed large amounts of dissolved $\mathrm{AlF_3}$ and, below $700^{\circ}\mathbf{C}$ , composition changes which indicated the separation of a fluoride phase rich in LiF, $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ , and $\mathrm{AlF_3}$ , and containing some cerium. The instability of $\mathrm{Al_2O_3}$ plus the fact that the $\mathrm{Al_2O_3 - SiO_2}$ phase diagram shows no high-melting intermediate phase suggests that zeolite $(\mathrm{Al_2O_3 - SiO_2})$ ion exchangers would also be unstable in the presence of molten fluorides.

While thus far these tests have revealed no oxide material which could act as an effective agent for rare-earth removal from molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ fluorides, $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ shows enough promise to warrant further study. Another important result is that we have confirmed the expected stability of these oxides in the presence of molten fluorides. $\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}$ , even though of no apparent use as an extractant for rare earths, may prove useful as a container or insulator material for MSBR fluorides. We have not exhausted the list of oxide materials which form double oxides with rare earths and which might be stable or nearly stable in molten fluorides. Among the oxides soon to be tested, in addition to further tests of $\mathbf{V}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ , are $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ , $\mathrm{ZrO_2}$ ,

$\mathrm{Nb}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ $\mathrm{Ta}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{5}$ , and $\mathrm{MoO_2}$ . In these tests rare earths heavier than cerium (whose tracer $^{144}\mathrm{Ce}$ we have used as an analytical convenience) will be present, since these should have a greater tendency to form the desired double-oxide phases.

# 13.3 LIQUIDUS TEMPERATURES IN THE SYSTEM LiF-BeF2-ThF4

C.J.Barton L.O.Gilpatrick H.Insley

During the course of fractional crystallization studies of $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}\cdot \mathrm{ThF_4}\cdot \mathrm{CeF_3}$ mixtures described in the previous report, it was observed that the liquidus temperature of the mixture having the composition $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}\cdot \mathrm{ThF_4}$ (60-28-12 mole $\%$ , average of four analyses; calculated composition 58-30-12 mole $\%$ ) was at least $50^{\circ}C$ higher than predicted by the published phase diagram. It seemed worth while, therefore, to redetermine liquidus temperatures of several compositions in this system that have been, or may be, of interest for use in breeder reactors. The measurements were made by use of the gradient quenching and DTA techniques. The results are recorded in Table 13.4. The liquidus values for the 72 and 68 mole $\%$ LiF mixtures, in the composition range presently favored for MSBR use, are in fair agreement with literature values. However, the new values for the 64 and 58 mole $\%$ LiF compositions show that they are not suitable for reactor use and that the range of $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ solvent compositions for such use is not as wide as the published phase diagram indicates.

The phases observed in the quenched samples agreed, in general, with the predictions of the published diagram, but some thermal effects were observed in the DTA curves that could not be explained by the quenching data. This is probably due to the fact that the quenching technique gives equilibrium data, whereas DTA is a dynamic process.

# 13.4 THE SOLUBILITY OF THORIUM IN THORIUM TETRAFLUORIDE

A.S.Dworkin M.A.Bredig

Thermal analysis measurements were made to determine the eutectic temperature in the Th-ThF4 system, and through combination with the entropy of fusion of ThF4, the solubility of Th in ThF4. The eutectic temperature was found to be only 1 to $2^{\circ}$ lower than the melting point $(1110^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ of ThF4, indicating a solubility of about 0.1 to 0.2 mole % Th in ThF4. This is of the same low order of magnitude as the solubility in 3LiF-ThF4 and as the solubilities of La, Ce, and Nd in their respective trifluorides, which we had measured earlier.

The low solubility of Th in $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ indicates that the formation of a stable trivalent thorium fluoride in any appreciable concentration is unlikely.

# 13.5 POTENTIOMETRIC DETERMINATION OF THE $\mathbf{U}^{4 + } / \mathbf{U}^{3 + }$ RATIO IN MOLTEN FLUORIDES

B.F.Hitch C.F.Baes,Jr.

The $\mathbf{U}^{4 + } / \mathbf{U}^{3 + }$ couple has been compared as previously described with the Ni,NiO,BeO reference electrode in the following cell:

$$
\mathrm {P t} (c) \left| \begin{array}{l} \mathrm {U F} _ {4}, \mathrm {U F} _ {3}, \\ \mathrm {L i F}, \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} \end{array} \right| \left\| \begin{array}{l} \mathrm {L i F} (0. 6 7), \\ \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} (0. 3 3), \\ \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\sim 1 0 ^ {- 4}) \end{array} \right| \tag {I}
$$

The Ni, NiO, BeO reference electrode was compartmented by using a silica liner inserted in a nickel tube. The silica and nickel tubes contained frits of $\sim 10\mu$ pore size. Experiments so far have been carried out in the two fluoride mixtures LiF-BeF2-UF4 (66-33-1 mole $\%$ ) and LiF-BeF2-ZrF4-UF4 (64-32-3-1). The time required for the reference electrode to reach a stable potential, once the reference electrode assembly is immersed in the melt, is 1 to 4 hr. Although the silica compartments are slowly attacked by the fluoride melts, they usually last from two to four weeks.

Measurements were made at $610^{\circ}$ by reducing $\mathbf{U}^{4+}$ to $\mathbf{U}^{3+}$ with either beryllium or zirconium metal, then oxidizing with weighed increments of anhydrous $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ . As indicated by the cell potential, a period of about 30 min was required for complete reaction of a typical 40-mg addition of $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ . In earlier experiments we tried to reduce the $\mathbf{U}^{4+}$ to $\mathbf{U}^{3+}$ incrementally by the addition of weighed amounts of beryllium or zirconium metal to the melt; however, beryllium additions gave erratic results, and while zirconium gave more reproducible results, the time required for complete reaction of zirconium with the melt was often several hours. It is possible that in these early experiments some of our difficulties may have been caused by beryllium or zirconium metal alloying with the nickel container vessel. Hence, for the initial reduction of melts in the present measurements, we have followed a procedure of carefully exposing the beryllium or zirconium metal to the melt without touching the container walls and then removing it when the desired potential was reached.

Results of several oxidation runs are shown as plots of $\log (X_{\mathbf{UF}_3} / X_{\mathbf{UF}_4})$ vs the cell potential $E_{1}$ in Fig. 13.1. The initial value of the ratio $X_{\mathbf{UF}_3} / X_{\mathbf{UF}_4}$ in each series of measurements was adjusted so that subsequent, lower ratios derived from the amounts of $\mathrm{NiF_2}$ oxidant added produced plots which were, as nearly as possible, linear and of the theoretical slope expected from the simple Nernst expression

$$
E _ {1} = E _ {1} ^ {\prime} - \frac {R T}{F} \ln \frac {X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}}{X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}}}. \tag {1}
$$

This procedure was based on the assumption that the $\mathrm{NiF_2}$ added had oxidized only $\mathrm{UF_3}$ and was consumed by no other reducing agent. With the precautions cited above regarding the use of beryllium and zirconium metal in the initial reductions, this appeared to be a valid assumption since a suitable adjustment of the initial $X_{\mathrm{UF}_3} / X_{\mathrm{UF}_4}$ ratio did indeed produce linear plots of the expected slope. The plots for all runs at both melt compositions were essentially the same, giving

$$
E _ {\mathbf {I}} ^ {\prime} = 1. 0 6 7 \mathbf {v}
$$

at $610^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$

If in this cell we assume, as previously, $^{9}$ that all the current is carried across the liquid junction by the $\mathbf{L}\mathbf{i}^{+}$ ions, we may write the following reaction:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {N i O} (c) + \mathrm {U F} _ {3} (d) + \mathrm {L i F} (d) + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} (d, \mathrm {R}) \\ \rightarrow \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {N i} (c) + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {B e O} (c) + \mathrm {U F} _ {4} (d) + \mathrm {L i F} (d, \mathrm {R}), \tag {1} \\ \end{array}
$$

![](images/3487dee12864b5bee4c0a320f06a49c9cb770a2c0feeab94dc77d3a3411c135c.jpg)  
Fig. 13.1. Potential of Cell I vs the ratio of $\mathbf{U}^{3+}$ to $\mathbf{U}^{4+}$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ Melts.

and hence the following expression for $E_{\mathbf{I}}$

$$
\begin{array}{l} E _ {1} = E _ {1} ^ {\infty} - \frac {R T}{F} \ln \frac {X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}}{X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}}} - \frac {R T}{F} \ln \frac {\gamma_ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}}{\gamma_ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}}} \\ \cdot \frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {L i F}}} \cdot \frac {a _ {\mathrm {L i F} (\mathrm {R})}}{a _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2} (\mathrm {R})} ^ {1 / 2}}, \tag {2} \\ \end{array}
$$

where R designates solutes in the reference compartment. Since in the present measurements the reference solution closely approximated the composition 2LiF-BeF, the standard state which we have adopted for $\operatorname {LiF}(d)$ and $\mathrm{BeF}_2(d)$ , then $a_{\mathrm{LiF(R)}}$ and $a_{\mathrm{BeF_2(R)}}$ are unity. Thus we obtain the following expression for the observed value of $E_{1}^{\circ}$ , the cell potential corresponding to a unit ratio $\mathbf{U}^{4 + } / \mathbf{U}^{3 + }$ ..

$$
E _ {1} ^ {\prime} = E _ {1} ^ {\circ} - \frac {R T}{F} \ln \frac {\gamma_ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}}{\gamma_ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}}} \cdot \frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {L i F}}} \tag {3}
$$

Since the ratio $\gamma_{\mathbf{UF}_4} / \gamma_{\mathbf{UF}_3}^{10}$ and the activity of $\mathbf{LiF}^{11}$ are expected to change in opposite directions as the composition of the solution - and therefore the

free fluoride ion concentration - is changed, we would expect $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ to show an appreciable difference at the two melt compositions studied. Indeed, on estimating the changes expected in $\gamma_{\mathrm{UF_4}} / \gamma_{\mathrm{UF_3}}$ from the measurements by Long<sup>10</sup> of the reaction

$$
\mathrm {U F} _ {4} (d) + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {H} _ {2} (g) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {U F} _ {3} (d) + \mathrm {H F} (g) \tag {II}
$$

and the changes in $a_{\mathbf{LiF}}$ expected from our measurements of the cell reaction<sup>11</sup>

$$
2 \mathrm {H F} (g) + \operatorname {B e} (c) \rightarrow \operatorname {B e F} _ {2} (d) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} (g), \tag {III}
$$

we would expect $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ for the two melts to differ by $\sim 38$ mv. The absence of a detectable difference in $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ for the two melts suggests that they both have about the same free fluoride ion activity, which is quite surprising since the presence of 3 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{ZrF_4}$ in one melt is expected to appreciably lower the concentration of free fluoride ion. The effect, or lack of effect, of melt composition on $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ will be examined further.

Another surprising result is that the observed value of $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ differs some 85 mv (Fig. 13.1) from the values of $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\circ}$ which may be derived by combining the measurements for reactions II, III, and the following previously reported cell reaction:

$$
\operatorname {B e} (c) + \operatorname {N i O} (c) \rightarrow \operatorname {B e O} (c) + \operatorname {N i} (c). \tag {IV}
$$

Thus $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\circ}$ should be given by

$$
E _ {\mathrm {I}} ^ {\circ} = E _ {\mathrm {I V}} ^ {\circ} - E _ {\mathrm {I I I}} ^ {\circ} - E _ {\mathrm {I I}} ^ {\circ} = 1. 2 9 1 - 0. 1 5 7 T / 1 0 ^ {3}, \tag {4}
$$

and at $610^{\circ}\mathbf{C}E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\circ}$ should be 1.147 v. Since one of the present melt compositions is quite close to 2LiF-BeF, wherein $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ must equal $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\circ}$ by definition, the difference between the observed value of $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\prime}$ and the predicted value of $E_{\mathbf{I}}^{\circ}$ cannot be attributed to medium effects alone. This discrepancy prompted us to recheck our previous potentiometric measurements of cell IV, $^9$

$$
\mathrm {B e} ^ {\circ} \left[ \text {L i F - B e F} _ {2} \right] \left[ \text {L i F , B e F} _ {2} \right] \left[ \text {N i O} (c) \right. \left. \text {B e O} (c) \right] \left[ \text {N i} ^ {\circ} \quad . \quad (\mathrm {V}) \right.
$$

The results over a temperature range of 500 to $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ confirmed our earlier data. Thus we have no explanation for the inconsistency but plan to investigate it further.

Measurements of cell I so far indicate that the Ni, NiO, BeO electrode should be a satisfactory reference electrode for the potentiometric determination of the $\mathbf{U}^{4 + } / \mathbf{U}^{3 + }$ ratio in LiF-BeF $_2$ melts. The reproducibility thus far obtained (Fig. 13.1) suggests that accuracy of

the order of $\pm 5\%$ can be attained in the determination of this ratio once the value of $E^{\prime}$ has been established accurately from known $\mathrm{U}^{4 + } / \mathrm{U}^{3 + }$ ratios in the melt composition of interest.

# 13.6 REFINEMENT OF THE UF<sub>3</sub> SPECTRUM

L.M.Toth

Successful development of diamond-windowed spectrophotometric cells $^{12}$ has enabled us to maintain stable molten salt solutions of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ and thereby to initiate a study of the absorption spectra in LiF-BeF $_2$ - $\mathrm{UF}_3$ melts. The spectrum of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 mole %) was investigated with the concentration of $\mathrm{UF}_3 \cong 0.3$ mole %. The spectrum of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ from 650 to $2500\mathrm{m}\mu$ was found to agree well with the set of values reported previously. $^{13}$ The measured absorption coefficient $^{14}$ at $890\mathrm{m}\mu$ was 40 liters $\mathrm{mole}^{-1}\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ (cf. $\epsilon_{890} \cong 42$ previously $^{13}$ ).

The region from 250 to $650\mathrm{m}\mu$ , however, does not agree with the previous $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_3$ spectrum. The peak positions are identical, but their intensities are greater by approximately a factor of 2. In particular $\epsilon_{360}$ was found to be 860 liters mole-1 cm-1 (cf. $\epsilon_{360}\cong 440$ liters mole-1 cm-1).13

This new value of $\epsilon_{360} = 860$ liters mole $^{-1}$ cm $^{-1}$ is consistent with what is expected by comparing spectra of $\mathbf{U}^{3+}$ in nonfluoride systems. $^{13}$ This refinement of $\pmb{\epsilon}$ at $360~\mathrm{m}\mu$ was achieved by comparing relative absorbances at 360 and $890~\mathrm{m}\mu$ in the same spectrum. Thus, using Beer's law,

$$
\frac {A _ {3 6 0}}{A _ {8 9 0}} = \frac {\epsilon_ {3 6 0} \rho}{\epsilon_ {8 9 0} \rho};
$$

$A_{360} / A_{890}$ was measured, and $\epsilon_{890}$ was calculated from concentrated $\mathrm{UF}_3$ solutions. With these values $\epsilon_{360}$ was calculated from the above equation.

The importance of measuring relative absorbances of the two peaks should be stressed. A spectrum that checks in this manner is referred to here as internally consistent. The details of the method used as well as the corrected $\mathrm{UF}_3$ spectrum will be reported later.

Table 13.5. U(III) Concentration as Percent of Total U   

<table><tr><td colspan="4">Spectrophotometric</td><td>Potentiometric</td><td>Voltammetric</td></tr><tr><td>Peaks Useda in Determination (mμ)</td><td>Old Value</td><td>Corrected Value</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>a</td><td>360</td><td>&lt;0.2</td><td>&lt;0.1</td><td>0.06</td><td>0.01</td></tr><tr><td>b</td><td>360</td><td>1.5</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.9-1.1</td><td>0.7-1.0</td></tr><tr><td>c</td><td>360</td><td>3.5</td><td>1.75</td><td>1.7-1.8</td><td>1.1-1.5</td></tr><tr><td>d</td><td>890</td><td>12.0</td><td>12.0</td><td>9.7</td><td>10.0</td></tr></table>

$\pmb{a}$ Private communication with J.P. Young.

This correction is of particular MSR interest because of its effect on $\mathrm{UF_3 / UF_4}$ ratios measured spectrophotometrically. Applying the correction to $\mathrm{UF_3 / UF_4}$ determinations where the $360\text{-m}\mu$ peak was used would reduce the previous values to approximately $\frac{1}{2}$ their value. For example, Table 15.1 of Sect. 15.2 (this report) would be corrected as shown in Table 13.5.

In every case where the $360\text{-}\mathfrak{m}\mu$ peak was used (values $a, b,$ and $c$ ), better agreement is achieved by applying the correction. Although the value for $a$ was below the detection limits of the instrument,[15] the correction is still legitimate. Furthermore it demonstrates that the sensitivity limit of the spectrophotometric method is at least one-half that which would be anticipated from the old value in the table. No correction was applied to $d$ because the $890\text{-}\mathfrak{m}\mu$ peak was used. The net result of the $360\text{-}\mathfrak{m}\mu$ absorption coefficient correction is better agreement of the spectrophotometric method with the other two methods.

Base-line uncertainty and light scatter can account for approximately $10\%$ error in $\epsilon_{360} = 860$ . Further refinement within this $10\%$ uncertainty is foreseen and will be reported as these sources of error are examined.

# 13.7 EMF MEASUREMENTS WITH CONCENTRATION CELLS WITH TRANSFERENCE IN MOLTEN MIXTURES OF LiF AND $\mathbf{BeF}_2$

K.A.Romberger J.Braunstein

As part of a general investigation orientated toward characterizing ionic mobilities in fused salts, emf measurements are being made in the LiF-BeF $_2$ system which employ the concentration cell with transference

$$
\mathrm {B e} \left| \begin{array}{l l} \mathrm {L i F} & \mathrm {L i F} \\ \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} & \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} \\ \mathrm {I} & \mathrm {I I} \end{array} \right| \quad \text {B e}. \tag {1}
$$

The initial results from these measurements were reported in the last report of this series.16 Therein emf data $(E_{t})$ were reported for 500 and $610^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ which were obtained with an all-silica cell. The results over a limited range suggested that the transference number of lithium ion relative to fluoride ion was unity.

Measurements have now been extended over wider ranges of composition and temperature employing both silica and recently developed all-metal cells. The sensitivity, stability, and ruggedness of the latter have been demonstrated during several lengthy experiments. New results include:

1. Isothermal measurements with silica apparatus at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for compositions between 0.90 and 0.30 mole fraction $\mathbf{BeF}_2$ .   
2. Two different sets of isothermal measurements at $610^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ utilizing an all-metal (nickel, molybdenum) cell which covered compositions between 0.70 and the LiF liquidus at 0.267 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ .   
3. Measurements along the LiF liquidus between 610 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ using the metal cell.   
4. Isothermal measurements at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and constant composition measurements at temperatures between $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and the liquidus temperatures for $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ and $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{BeF}_4$ for compositions between 0.60 and 0.33 mole fraction $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ . The latter experiment also utilized the metal cell and is still in progress.

We have noted previously $^{16}$ that the emf data $(E_{w})$ obtained by Hitch and Baes $^{17}$ for the cell

Table 13.6. EMF's of Cells With Transference $(E_{t})$ , EMF's of Cells Without Transference $(E_{w})$ , and Lithium Ion Transference Numbers $(t_{Li})$ for the System LiF-BeF $_2$

aFrom B.F.Hitch and C.F.Baes,Inorg.Chem.8,201 (1969).   
$b_{\text{Emf}}$ decreased with time at higher LiF concentrations, possibly from attack on the silica. Error could be $\pm 5 \, \text{mv}$ .   
cEmf was erratic near 0.70 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . Error could be $\pm 3$ mv.

$$
\mathrm {P t}, \mathrm {H} _ {2}, \mathrm {H F} \left| \begin{array}{l} \mathrm {L i F} \\ \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} \end{array} \right| \mathrm {B e} \tag {2}
$$

could be combined with our emf values $(E_{t})$ at any one composition to determine the transport numbers of lithium and beryllium ions $(t_{\mathrm{Li}}, t_{\mathrm{Be}})$ relative to a fluoride ion reference through relations such as:

$$
d E _ {t} = t _ {\mathrm {L i}} \left(\frac {1 + x}{1 - x}\right) d E _ {w}, \tag {3a}
$$

$$
t _ {\mathrm {L i}} = \left(\frac {1 - x}{1 + x}\right) \frac {d E _ {t}}{d E _ {w}} = \left(\frac {1 - x}{1 + x}\right) \frac {d E _ {t} / d x}{d E _ {w} / d x}, \tag {3b}
$$

or for a composition interval between $a$ and $b$ ,

$$
\bar {t} _ {\mathrm {L i}} = \frac {E _ {t} ^ {b} - E _ {t} ^ {a}}{\int_ {b} ^ {a} [ (1 + x) / (1 - x) ] d E _ {w}}. \tag {4}
$$

Here $x$ is the mole fraction of beryllium fluoride. Our previous measurements showed that $\overline{t}_{\mathbf{L1}}$ equaled $1.00 \pm 0.05$ for compositions between 0.3 and 0.5 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ at both temperatures. This result has been confirmed by our subsequent measurements, not only for 500 and $610^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ but for $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ as well.

The results are shown in more detail in Table 13.6, where the values of $t_{\mathbf{Li}}$ for discrete composition intervals have been calculated at various temperatures. The uncertainty in $\overline{t}_{\mathbf{Li}}$ between 0.3 and 0.5 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ is about $\pm 5\%$ , while the uncertainty between 0.5 and 0.7 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ is about $\pm 20\%$ . Values are not tabulated for $t_{\mathbf{Li}}$ above 0.7 mole fraction

$\mathrm{BeF}_2$ because the relative error in $\Delta E_{w}$ is too large to give meaningful results. The $E_{t}$ values obtained recently at 610 and $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with metal cells reproduce closely the data obtained earlier at the same temperatures with silica cells. The conclusion is that the materials of construction of the cells did not influence the values of $E_{t}$ . This is remarkable in that the nickel compartments (which were electrically isolated from the rest of the cell) always had potentials which were positive (noble) relative to the beryllium electrodes from 0.4 to $1.2\mathrm{v}$ . The smallest potential difference which has been seen between a beryllium electrode and the separating (metal) compartment is over $300\mathrm{mv}$ . Silica frits were of much larger pore size ( $\sim 150\mu$ diameter) than the nickel frits ( $\sim 10\mu$ and $\sim 40\mu$ ), showing that for this range of pore diameters, $E_{t}$ was also independent of pore diameter.

Metal cells have a decided advantage over silica cells at higher LiF compositions ( $x_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ less than 0.3) since they are stable to the fluoride salts while silica is not. Moreover, the metal cells can be frozen without concentration change or damage, whereas the silica cells break upon freezing. Using metal cells we have made measurements along the LiF liquidus up to $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the upper temperature limit of the furnace. We plan to make more measurements in this high-LiF region in the future, and the data which are presently available will be presented at that time.

Metal cells have also yielded more precise emf values than have silica cells for values of $x_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ below $\sim 0.55$ . This precision is illustrated in Fig. 13.2, wherein data are shown which were obtained between 365 and

![](images/7def4a04008562366f20c2fe1469ac5f05ba2b28bdda3fd44130d98f2665c1b1.jpg)  
Fig. 13.2. $E_{t}$ vs Temperature for the LiF-BeF $_2$ System Near the $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ Liquidus. Reference composition: $\square$ , 0.5278 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . Total bulk compositions: $\triangledown$ , 0.5133; $\triangle$ , 0.5017; $\square$ , 0.4755; $\triangle$ , 0.4475; and $\bullet$ 0.4267 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . The points, $\bigcirc$ , are LiF addition points at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

$500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for compositions near the eutectic of the $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ system. The reference composition (II) is the same throughout, $x_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} = 0.5278$ . The labeled lines in the homogeneous region identify the composition in the bulk (I); the numerous points which form the boundary (liquidus) curve where $\mathrm{Li_2BeF_4}$ begins to precipitate have been derived from the various total bulk compositions, and there is considerable overlap. Since this boundary curve represents an invariant composition point at any one temperature, it forms an internal composition reference. If there is diffusion or flow from the bulk into the reference compartment, any unknown "aging" effects which affect the potential, or an inability of the stirrer to maintain thermal equilibrium in the presence of the precipitated solid, the observed emf will deviate from the line. These measurements were made over a period of two weeks, with the entire system frozen six times in between. No deviation was observed until the 0.5278-0.4267 composition couple was followed down to $392^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Here the deviation is $\sim 1.1$ mv or $0.8^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . At this point, it is calculated that $40\%$ of the total LiF and $27\%$ of the total $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ in the bulk compartment were present as precipitated $\mathrm{Li_2BeF_4}$ . For the same concentration set the deviation at $382^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ was about $1.1^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ or $1.0\mathrm{mv}$ . Here, $49\%$ of the total LiF and $33\%$ of the total $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ were present as a solid precipitate. The temperature at which the emf-temperature plot for any one composi

tion set intersects the invariant boundary curve is the liquidus temperature at that composition. These intersection points are obtainable with great precision, usually about $\pm 0.2^{\circ}\mathbf{C}$ . The values for these liquidus data are now being determined. They will be presented in the next volume of this series.

Although the general purpose of these emf studies is to aid in characterizing ionic transport properties, the excellent reproducibility and long-term stability of the all-metal cells indicate that they should have general applicability to several types of problems. These include:

1. Very precise determination of liquidus curves in binary, and possibly ternary, systems. We are pursuing this application as is demonstrated by Fig. 13.2.   
2. Routine analytical monitoring of compositions of binary salts. For example, with the $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ system between about $x_{\mathrm{BeF_2}} = 0.40$ and the LiF liquidus, $E_{t}$ changes approximately $14\mathrm{mv}$ per mole percent composition change. If a reference solution was saturated with LiF (i.e., its composition would be fixed) at any one temperature, then an emf uncertainty even as large as $\pm 1.4\mathrm{mv}$ would yield compositions precise to $\pm 0.001$ mole fraction.   
3. Reproducible and stable reference electrodes. Beryllium rod electrodes, immersed in melts of the same composition, in all-metal cells invariably show emf

differences of less than $0.3\mathrm{mv}$ .Usually the emf differences are no more than $0.05\mathrm{mv}$

# 13.8 IMPROVED DETERMINATION OF ELECTRICAL CONDUCTANCE OF LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 MOLE %)

G. D. Robbins J. Braunstein

The first determination of specific conductance in the current program of investigating electrical conductivities in systems of interest to the MSRP was that of the MSRE coolant salt, $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ (66-34 mole $\%$ ). Using a silica cell of refined design and an ac series-component Wheatstone-type bridge (both previously described $^{19}$ ), a more accurate determination of specific conductance over an extended temperature range has been obtained. These measurements extend the temperature range investigated to $470 - 645^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

Starting material $^{20}$ which had been previously treated with $\mathbf{H}_2 / \mathbf{HF}$ was remelted and filtered $^{21}$ under a helium atmosphere. The frozen salt was subsequently loaded into the silica conductance cell in a dry box and melted under dried helium. The sample melted completely clear, remained transparent for more than $13\mathrm{hr}$ , appeared somewhat milky after $22\mathrm{hr}$ , and was cloudy after two days. The open circle data shown in Fig. 13.3 were taken in random order during this period and demonstrate the absence of effects from oxide formed during the experiment.

Employing a silica cell of cell constant $150.42\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ and a peak-to-peak applied voltage of $25\mathrm{mv}$ across the cell, measured resistance was independent of measuring frequency over the range 5 to $50\mathrm{kHz}$ within the accuracy limits of the bridge $(\pm 0.2\%)$ . This was the first fluoride melt to show no frequency dispersion, probably indicative of its high purity.

Specific conductance data are given in Table 13.7. The variation of specific conductance with temperature appears linear over the temperature interval investi

# 13.9 ELECTRICAL CONDUCTIVITIES OF LOW-MELTING NaF-BeF $_2$ MIXTURES

G. D. Robbins J. Braunstein

The Vogel-Tammann-Fulcher (VTF) equation as applied to equivalent conductance, $\Lambda$ , in the form

$$
\ln \Lambda = A - \frac {1}{2} \ln T - \frac {k}{T - T _ {0}}
$$

has been successfully employed in binary nitrate melts to correlate the composition and temperature dependence of electrical conductivity.22-24 In this relation $T$ is the absolute temperature, while $A$ , $k$ , and $T_0$ are adjustable parameters. In the molten $\mathrm{KNO}_3\cdot \mathrm{Ca}(\mathrm{NO}_3)_2$ system Angell22,23 was able to determine $T_0$ , the zero-mobility temperature, for three glass-forming compositions by analyzing the curvature in plots of $\ln \Lambda$ vs $1 / T$ at temperatures below $2T_0$ . Employing the observed linear relationship between $T_0$ and mole fraction for these three compositions, $T_0$ was extrapolated to other compositions. The VTF equation was then found to adequately represent the data employing a single value of $k$ , independent of composition. The extent to which this behavior obtains in other systems is important in the correlation of electrical conductance with composition. To test the applicability of the VTF equation in correlating conductivities in molten fluorides, an attempt has been made to determine $T_0$ for three low-melting mixtures in the molten NaF-BeF2 system.

The previously described ac Wheatstone-type bridge, incorporating a series-component balancing arm, and the type of silica conductance cells used in studies of the LiF-BeF $_2$ system $^{25}$ were employed in these experiments. In the LiF-BeF $_2$ system it was possible to use silica cells with the molten fluoride, due in part to the slight solubility of BeO and low equilibrium partial

pressure of $\mathrm{SiF_4}$ formed via the corrosion reaction26

$$
2 \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} + \underline {{\mathrm {S i O} _ {2}}} \rightleftharpoons 2 \mathrm {B e O} + \mathrm {S i F} _ {4} \uparrow .
$$

A similar procedure has been followed with the NaF-BeF $_2$ system. The cell constant was determined to be $92.96 \, \text{cm}^{-1}$ ( $\pm 0.15\%$ precision) relative to molten potassium nitrate as a reference (accuracy = $\pm 0.5\%$ ). In all measurements the peak-to-peak voltage applied across the conductance cell was maintained at $25 \, \text{mv}$ , and the measured resistance was independent of the measuring frequency over the range 5 to $50 \, \text{kHz}$ , within the accuracy limits of the bridge ( $\pm 0.2\%$ ).

Hand-picked, glass-clear crystals of recrystallized NaF and sublimed $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ were melted under an inert atmosphere in the conductance cell. Semiquantitative spectrographic analysis of the starting materials showed the major impurities to be (in ppm by weight) for $\mathrm{BeF}_2: 50$ Li, $100\mathrm{Na}$ and for NaF: $2\mathrm{Mg}, 2\mathrm{Fe},$ and $3\mathrm{Ca}$ .

The phase diagram $^{28,29}$ for the system NaF-BeF $_2$ shows two low-melting eutectics (340 and $365^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) at 43 and 55 mole % beryllium fluoride, with the compound NaBeF $_3$ congruently melting at $376^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Specific conductances have been determined as functions of temperature for these three compositions and are shown in Fig. 13.4. Data are given in Table 13.8. The data were taken in random order over several days, at each composition, thus demonstrating the absence of time or corrosion effects on the results. The curves shown were calculated from computer-fitted least-squares equations similar to the VTF equation. The upper portion of Table 13.9 lists the parameter values, together with the standard parameter errors and the standard errors of fit.

Density data from ref. 30 were converted to molar volumes and computer fitted by linear expressions in mole fraction at constant temperature. Discarding the disparate data at 50 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ , the linear least-squares equations of molar volume as a function of composition at various temperatures were employed to interpolate to the desired compositions. Densities were obtained by linear interpolation for the three compositions of interest (the temperature variation of the

![](images/be96bd5f1773ce9dd88d36d895889c57fbf6666867741deed5cc7f7543c42a37.jpg)  
Fig. 13.4. Specific Conductance vs Temperature $(^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ for NaF-BeF $_2$ Mixtures.

Table 13.8. Specific Conductance of NaF-BeF $_2$ Mixtures   
Table 13.9. Parameter Values for Computer-Fitted Equations

$$
\ln \kappa = B - \frac {1}{2} \ln T - \frac {C}{T - D}
$$

densities appearing more nearly linear than molar volumes in this temperature interval). The equations employed in the interpolation are given in Table 13.10. The densities were combined with the specific conductance data to calculate equivalent conductances,

$$
\Lambda = \kappa \cdot \frac {\text {e q u i v a l e n t w e i g h t}}{\text {d e n s i t y}}.
$$

Equivalent weights were calculated as

equivalent weight $= 0.5N_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}M_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} + N_{\mathrm{NaF}}M_{\mathrm{NaF}}$

where $N$ and $M$ with subscripts refer to equivalent fraction and formula weight respectively.

![](images/801402d4705bcd14e8737494998db84f69ca1ec3504b228b338da2f86ccebacd.jpg)  
Fig. 13.5. Logarithm Equivalent Conductance vs Reciprocal Temperature $(^{\circ}\mathbf{K})$ for NaF-BeF $_2$ Mixtures.

Plots of In $\Lambda$ vs reciprocal temperature $(^{\circ}\mathbf{K})$ are presented in Fig. 13.5. Results of least-squares fit of the VTF equation are shown in the center section of Table 13.9. The curves in Fig. 13.5 are from these equations. Comparison of $T_{0}$ with the parameter $D$ in the VTF analog of the specific conductance equation for the three compositions reveals the effect of incorporating density data in the analysis.

The Adam-Gibbs probability expression, $W(T)$ , for cooperative rearrangement, $^{31,24}$

$$
W (T) = A ^ {\prime} - \frac {k ^ {\prime}}{T \ln T / T _ {0} ^ {\prime}},
$$

differs from the VTF equation by the additive term $-\frac{1}{2}\ln T$ (which has little effect) and the approximation

$$
T \ln T / T _ {0} ^ {\prime} \simeq T - T _ {0}
$$

as $T$ approaches $T_0$ . To determine the effect of these differences in the temperature range encompassed by the data, expressions of the form

$$
\ln \Lambda = A ^ {\prime} - \frac {k ^ {\prime}}{T \ln T / T _ {0} ^ {\prime}}
$$

were also fitted. These results are shown in the lower portion of Table 13.9. Both the VTF and Adam-Gibbs expressions fit the data equally well. In both the VTF equation and the Adam-Gibbs probability expression $A(A^{\prime})$ is relatively constant, $k(k^{\prime})$ exhibits similar values for the two compositions richer in $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , and the values

Table 13.11. $T_0$ Estimates Based on Lower-Temperature Data   

<table><tr><td>Mole % BeF2</td><td>T0</td><td>σ(fit)</td></tr><tr><td>43</td><td>258 (±165)</td><td>0.0096</td></tr><tr><td>50</td><td>387 (±22)</td><td>0.0016</td></tr><tr><td>55</td><td>299 (±43)</td><td>0.0041</td></tr></table>

of $T_{0}(T_{0}^{\prime})$ are similar in magnitude to $T_{0}$ obtained in an LiF-BeF2 mixture.25 Although the three NaF-BeF2 values of $T_{0}(T_{0}^{\prime})$ can be fitted by a line through the error limits when plotted vs composition, there are as yet insufficient data to define the relation between $T_{0}$ and composition accurately. While $A^{\prime}$ and $k^{\prime}$ differ significantly from $A$ and $k$ , $T_{0}$ and $T_{0}^{\prime}$ are comparable in magnitude, and the variation of $T_{0}$ and $T_{0}^{\prime}$ with composition is somewhat similar.

Based on values of $T_{0}$ given in Table 13.9, the ratios of melting temperatures to $T_{0}$ (in degrees K) are 1.88, 2.13, and 2.05 for 43, 50, and 55 mole % BeF₂ respectively. Since it did not prove possible to supercool these mixtures, probably due to the presence of beryllium oxide, none of the data for 50 or 55 mole % BeF₂ fall in the temperature range less than $2T_{0}$ , while only 3 of the 11 data at 43 mole % are below $2T_{0}$ .

It might be assumed that the lower portions of the temperature ranges covered should give a more accurate - though less precise - estimate of $T_{0}$ . However, as Table 13.11 demonstrates, estimates of $T_{0}$ based on the lowest five data for each composition resulted in values of considerable uncertainty. Hence, one must consider the data in Table 13.9 the best estimates available for this system. That $k$ for two of the three compositions is similar is encouraging. However, the results demonstrate the necessity of further measurements in cells not subject to fluoride attack. In addition, experiments are currently in progress to determine the experimental glass transition temperatures of these mixtures by differential thermal analysis.

actual measurements of amplitudes and of oscillation periods and the initial treatment of the data were performed at Mound Laboratory, Miamisburg, Ohio, by L. J. Wittenberg and R. Dewitt. Preparation of samples, fabrication of capsules, and supplementary interpretation were done at ORNL.

The data, summarized in Table 13.12, were usually collected over about a $100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ temperature interval. The two salts composed of $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ exhibit viscosities rather close to salts where $\mathrm{UF_4}$ is substituted for $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ . The results for alkali fluoroborates are similar to those of alkali iodides at the same corresponding temperatures.

The equation given in Table 13.12 for $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ represents a redetermination of viscosity data; these newer data are approximately $50\%$ higher than preliminary data for $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ given in an earlier report.[33] The newer data (i.e., those given in Table 13.12) are believed to be more accurate, primarily because they agree with the viscosity data of 92-8 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF.

# 13.11 DENSITY OF MOLTEN FLUORIDES OF REACTOR INTEREST

Stanley Cantor

The densities of several fluoride mixtures were measured dilatometrically, $^{34}$ continuing the work reported previously. $^{35}$ The results are summarized in Table 13.13; the last three salt mixtures listed were also in the last semiannual report. $^{35}$

An interesting result, derived from the study of the three $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ -containing mixtures, was that expansivity (which is the fractional change of volume with temperature) showed little change with the concentrations of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ and of $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ . The results are given in Table 13.14. Assuming that the concentration of LiF in the fuel melt of an MSBR will be approximately 70 mole $\%$ then these results suggest that the MSBR fuel mixture will have an expansivity very close to $2.5\times 10^{-4} / ^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .
